Disagreement on Passing Wind and Direct Contact with Women
Disagreement on passing wind and ruling on direct contact with women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the dislike of passing wind in the mosque and state the ruling on direct contact with women as clearly forbidden.

Core Idea
Passing wind in the mosque is disputed; direct contact with women is unequivocally forbidden.

This lesson examines two additional acts related to mosque sanctity: passing wind (ريح) and direct contact with women. The text reports a disagreement (اختلف) on the former, while the latter is clearly stated as forbidden (حرام). Understanding this distinction helps differentiate between settled rulings and areas of scholarly difference.

Guided Path
1
Locate the disagreement on passing wind
Find the phrase 'واختلف في كراهة إخراج الريح فيه'. This explicitly says there is difference of opinion on whether it is disliked.
The passage does not resolve which view is stronger; it simply notes the ikhtilaf.
2
Understand the term 'تخلي'
Read 'والمراد من التخلي التغوط، وقيل: الخلوة بالمرأة'. This clarifies that 'تخلي' here means defecation, and one opinion interprets it as seclusion with a woman.
The minority interpretation may hint at the link between private acts and mosque adab.
3
Identify the ruling on direct contact with women
In the commentary ('Ayni), find 'مباشرة النساء في المسجد حرام'. This states that direct skin-to-skin contact with non-mahram women in the mosque is forbidden.
This is a clear and unconditional ruling within the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوطء فوقه ففي داخله بالأولى، وكره البول ولو في إناء، وينبغي لداخل المسجد أن يتعاهد نعله وخفه عن النجاسة، واختلف في كراهة إخراج الريح فيه والمراد من التخلي التغوط، وقيل: الخلوة بالمرأة، ولم يذكر المصنف كراهة البول والمجامعة في مصلى الجنازة، قال بعض أصحابنا يكره كما في المساجد التي على القوارع عند الحياض، والأصح أنه ليس له حرمة المسجد كمصلى العيد، والمختار للفتوى في مصلى الجنازة والعيد أنه مسجد في حق جواز الاقتداء، وفي ماعدا ذلك ليس له حكم المسجد. (فتح) فوقه أي فوق المسجد؛ لأن سطحه مسجد ولهذا يصح الاقتداء منه بمن فيه. (ع) لا فوق بيت إلخ: التقييد بـ"فوق" اتفاقي؛ لجواز المجامعة ودخول الجنب والحائض في مسجد البيت من غير كراهة، بخلاف سقف المسجد، فإنه لا يحل لهما الوقوف عليه وكذا مباشرة النساء في المسجد حرام ودخول الصبيان والمجانين حيث يغلب تنجيسهم، وإلا فيكره، وقيل: دخول المسجد متنعلاً من سوء أدب. (فتح) مسجد أي للصلاة، وهو ليس بمسجد حقيقة حتى يجوز بيعه فلم يكن له حرمة المساجد. (ع)
And treading on top of it, so in its interior it is even more [disliked]. And he disliked urination even in a vessel. And it is recommended for the one entering the mosque to check his sandal and leather socks for impurity. And there is disagreement on the dislike of passing wind in it. And the meaning of 'takhalli' is defecation. And it was said: privacy with a woman. And the author did not mention the dislike of urination and intercourse in the janazah musalla. Some of our companions said it is disliked as in the mosques that are on the main roads near the tanks. And the correct view is that it does not have the sanctity of the mosque like the 'Id musalla. And the chosen for fatwa regarding the janazah and 'id musalla is that it is a mosque regarding the validity of following (iqtida'), and regarding other than that it does not have the ruling of a mosque. (Fath) Above it, i.e., above the mosque, because its roof is a mosque, and for this reason following from it to those inside is valid. ('Ayni) 'Not above a house...': The restriction to 'above' is coincidental; because it is permissible to have intercourse and for a junub or menstruating woman to enter a mosque that is part of a house without dislike, unlike the roof of a mosque, for it is not permissible for them to stand upon it. Similarly, direct contact with women in the mosque is forbidden. And the entry of children and insane people who often make impurity is disliked, otherwise it is disliked. And it was said: entering the mosque with shoes is bad manners. (Fath) A mosque, i.e., for prayer, and it is not a mosque in reality such that its sale is permissible, so it does not have the sanctity of mosques. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of disliked acts (makruhat) related to mosques. It covers several rulings: First, treading on top of the mosque is disliked, and even more so inside. Second, urination is disliked even if done in a vessel. Third, one entering the mosque should check his footwear for impurity. Fourth, passing wind in the mosque is subject to disagreement; the text does not settle it. Fifth, 'takhalli' (relieving oneself) is defined as defecation. Sixth, the author did not address the dislikeness of urination and intercourse in the janazah musalla (prayer area for funeral). Some Hanafi scholars say it is disliked just like in regular mosques, but the correct view is that the janazah musalla does not have the full sanctity (hurmah) of a mosque, similar to the 'id musalla. However, for the sake of following the imam (iqtida'), it is considered a mosque. The roof of a mosque is considered part of the mosque, so following the imam from the roof is valid. A house that contains a mosque within it: acts like intercourse and entering by a junub or menstruating woman are not disliked in the house area, but the actual mosque area (even the roof) has sanctity. Direct contact with women in the mosque is forbidden. Children and insane people whose impurity is dominant should not enter; otherwise it is disliked. Entering with shoes is bad manners. Finally, a mosque that is a prayer room (not a full masjid appointed for all prayers) is not considered a complete mosque with full sanctity, so its sale is permissible, and it does not have the same hurmah as a dedicated mosque.

Key Terms
الريح (al-reeh): Passing wind from the anus (fart).
المباشرة (al-mubasharah): Direct skin-to-skin contact, especially between a man and woman who are not mahram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on direct contact with women in the mosque?
A) Disliked
B) Forbidden
C) Permissible if married
D) Subject to disagreement

Key Takeaway
Passing wind in the mosque is subject to ikhtilaf, while direct contact with women is clearly forbidden.

Status of Janazah and 'Id Musalla
Status of janazah and 'id musalla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the janazah musalla is a mosque only for the validity of iqtida', not for other rulings, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The janazah musalla is a mosque only for following the imam; all other mosque rules do not apply.

This lesson focuses on the legal status of the musalla used for janazah (funeral) and 'id prayers. The author reports a disagreement among Hanafi scholars: some treat it as a full mosque, while the adopted view (al-ashah) is that it only has mosque sanctity regarding following the imam (iqtida'). This nuanced ruling illustrates how Hanafi scholars balance the function of a place with its sacred designation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: the author's omission
Find 'ولم يذكر المصنف كراهة البول والمجامعة في مصلى الجنازة'. The author (Kanz al-Daqaiq) did not mention the dislike of urination and intercourse in the janazah musalla, implying it may have different rulings.
This omission leads to the following discussion on the musalla's status.
2
Understand the two opinions
First opinion: 'قال بعض أصحابنا يكره' – some Hanafi scholars say it is disliked just like in regular mosques. Second opinion: 'الأصح أنه ليس له حرمة المسجد كمصلى العيد' – the more correct view is that it does not have the sanctity of a mosque, like the 'id musalla.
The second view compares the janazah musalla to the 'id musalla, which is not a permanent masjid.
3
Extract the mufta bihi position
Read 'والمختار للفتوى في مصلى الجنازة والعيد أنه مسجد في حق جواز الاقتداء، وفي ماعدا ذلك ليس له حكم المسجد'. This is the chosen fatwa: it is a mosque only for iqtida', not for other rulings.
This is a classic example of partial sanctity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فتح) فوقه أي فوق المسجد؛ لأن سطحه مسجد ولهذا يصح الاقتداء منه بمن فيه. Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of disliked acts (makruhat) related to mosques. It covers several rulings: First, treading on top of the mosque is disliked, and even more so inside. Second, urination is disliked even if done in a vessel. Third, one entering the mosque should check his footwear for impurity. Fourth, passing wind in the mosque is subject to disagreement; the text does not settle it. Fifth, 'takhalli' (relieving oneself) is defined as defecation. Direct contact with women in the mosque is forbidden. Children and insane people whose impurity is dominant should not enter; otherwise it is disliked. Entering with shoes is bad manners. Finally, a mosque that is a prayer room (not a full masjid appointed for all prayers) is not considered a complete mosque with full sanctity, so its sale is permissible, and it does not have the same hurmah as a dedicated mosque.

Key Terms
مصلى الجنازة (musallā al-janāzah): A designated area for funeral prayers.
الاقتداء (al-iqtidā'): Following the imam in congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi ruling on the janazah musalla?
A) It has the full sanctity of a masjid
B) It is a masjid only for the validity of iqtida'
C) It is not a masjid under any circumstance
D) It has the same ruling as a regular mosque except for sale

Key Takeaway
The janazah and 'id musalla are considered mosques only for the purpose of following the imam in prayer, not for other rulings.

Roof, House, and Entry of Junub: Degrees of Mosque Sanctity
Rulings on roof, house mosque, and entry of various people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the sanctity of a mosque roof and a house that contains a mosque, and apply the rulings on entry of junub and menstruating women and children.

Core Idea
The roof of a mosque shares its sanctity; a house containing a mosque does not fully extend sanctity to the house.

This lesson continues the theme of partial sanctity, focusing on the mosque roof and a house that has a mosque inside. The text explains that the roof of a mosque is considered part of the mosque for rulings like iqtida', but for a house that contains a mosque, the house area does not have mosque sanctity. Additionally, it covers rulings on entry of junub, menstruating women, children, and madmen, and the etiquette of wearing shoes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the roof ruling
From the commentary: 'فوقه أي فوق المسجد؛ لأن سطحه مسجد' – The roof is considered part of the mosque. Therefore, following the imam from the roof is valid.
This applies to a dedicated mosque, not a musalla.
2
Contrast with a house containing a mosque
From 'Ayni: 'لا فوق بيت إلخ: التقييد بـ"فوق" اتفاقي؛ لجواز المجامعة ودخول الجنب والحائض في مسجد البيت من غير كراهة' – The restriction is coincidental; in a house mosque, intercourse and entry of junub/menstruating woman are not disliked because the house part is not sanctified.
The phrase 'اتفاقي' indicates that the ruling applies only to a full mosque roof, not to a house.
3
Identify rulings on children, madmen, and shoes
Find 'دخول الصبيان والمجانين حيث يغلب تنجيسهم، وإلا فيكره، وقيل: دخول المسجد متنعلاً من سوء أدب' – Entry of children and madmen who often make impurity is disliked; otherwise it is also disliked. Entering with shoes is bad manners.
These are matters of adab and precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المجانين (al-majānīn): Insane people, those lacking mental capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Haram, because the house is considered part of the mosque
B) Permissible, without dislike
C) Disliked
D) Permissible only if they perform ghusl first

Key Takeaway
Only the actual structure of a dedicated mosque has full sanctity; a house containing a mosque does not extend that sanctity to the entire house.

Plastering Mosques with Gypsum and Gold
Ruling on decorating mosques with gypsum and gold leaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on decorating mosques and identify when liability (daman) arises from using waqf funds.

Core Idea
Decorating a mosque is permissible from personal wealth, but incurs liability if funded by waqf money.

Mosque decoration is a topic of scholarly discussion. In this passage, we see the Hanafi view that beautification is at best permissible and even disliked, while strengthening the structure is commendable. Crucially, the source of funding determines whether the act is praiseworthy or brings legal liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and divergent opinions
Note the act: plastering (نقش) the mosque with gypsum and gold. Some say it is disliked (makruh), others consider it a good deed (qurba). The Hanafis allow it but do not commend it.
This sets the spectrum of opinions.
2
Extract the Hanafi preferred view
The commentator states that our companions allowed it but did not deem it commendable. The phrase 'لا يكره' indicates it is not severely prohibited, only permissible in principle.
The mufta bihi is permissibility without recommendation.
3
Distinguish between strengthening and decoration
The mutawalli (trustee) may do what strengthens the building, not what is purely for adornment. Strengthening is praiseworthy, decoration is at best neutral.
This nuance prevents wasteful spending on ornamentation.
4
Apply the ruling on funding source
If done from personal lawful wealth, it can be glorification of Allah. If from waqf funds, the person is liable (daman) because it misuses endowment property.
The source of money changes the legal consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نقشه بالجص إلخ: قيل: مكروه، وقيل: هي قربة، وأصحابنا جوزوه ولم يستحسنوه، فللمتولي أن يفعل ما يرجع إلى استحكام البناء دون ما يرجع إلى التزيين، وفي قوله: "لا يكره" إشارة إلى أنه لا يوجز بذلك، ويكفيه أن يجوز رأساً برأس؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عد من أشراط الساعة تزخرف المساجد وتطول المنارات. (فتح، مستخلص) بالجص: بالفتح والكسر، معرب، والعرب تسميه القص. (ط) الذهب والفضة واللازدرد ونحوها من الألوان؛ لأن في ذلك تعظيم بيت الله تعالى إذا فعل من مال نفسه الحلال، أما من مال الوقف فيضمن. (ط)
And [the author's statement] 'and not plastering it with gypsum etc.': It was said: it is disliked, and it was said: it is a good deed, and our companions allowed it but did not consider it commendable. So the person in charge may do that which pertains to strengthening the building, not that which pertains to decoration. And in his statement 'it is not disliked' is an indication that he does not consider it intensively prohibited, and it suffices that it is permissible in principle; because the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم counted among the signs of the Hour the decoration of mosques and the lengthening of minarets. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'By gypsum': with fatha and kasra, it is a loanword, and the Arabs call it 'al-qass'. (T) Gold, silver, lapis lazuli, and similar colors [are included] because in that is glorification of the House of Allah if done from one's own lawful wealth, but from the endowment (waqf) wealth, he will be liable for damages. (T)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on decorating mosques with gypsum (plaster) and gold. There are different opinions: some say it is disliked (makruh), others say it is a good deed (qurba). The Hanafi scholars allowed it but did not consider it commendable (mustahsan). The person in charge of the mosque (mutawalli) may do what strengthens the building, not what is purely for ornamentation. The author's phrase 'it is not disliked' indicates that it is not severely prohibited, but it is only permissible (ja'iz) in principle. This is because the Prophet mentioned that decoration of mosques and lengthening of minarets are signs of the Hour, implying disapproval. The passage also explains that if the decoration is done from one's own lawful wealth, it may be considered glorification of Allah's house, but if done from the mosque's endowment (waqf) money, the person will be liable (daman) because it is a misuse of waqf funds.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment for charitable purposes, the property of which cannot be sold or used for personal benefit.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to compensate for damage or misuse of property, especially waqf.
التزخرف (al-tazkhruf): Decoration or adornment, especially of mosques; referred to in the hadith as a sign of the Hour.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone uses waqf money to decorate the mosque with gold?
A) It is recommended
B) It is permissible without issue
C) He will be liable (daman) for the misappropriation
D) It is forbidden regardless

Key Takeaway
Decorating a mosque is permissible from personal wealth but incurs liability when funded by waqf money; strengthening the structure is preferred over decoration.

Salah — Prayer

The Rulings of Witr: Wajib or Sunnah? A Hanafi Analysis
A comprehensive fiqh ruling on Witr with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the obligation of Witr, explain the proofs for three rak'ahs with one taslim, and distinguish the Hanafi view on qunut from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Witr is wajib in the Hanafi school — a command and the term 'increase' prove it is of the same genus as fard.

The Witr prayer, performed after Isha, is a subject of significant scholarly debate. While many consider it merely recommended, Imam Abu Hanifa and his school hold it to be a distinct obligation (wajib) with specific rules: three rak'ahs with one taslim and qunut before ruku' throughout the year. This lesson unpacks the textual proofs, the intra-Hanafi disagreement with the Sahibayn, and the Shafi'i alternative, sharpening your ability to evaluate legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
What is the hukm of Witr? Is it wajib or sunnah? Recognize that within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa holds wajib while the Sahibayn say sunnah mu'akkada, and externally the Shafi'i school also says sunnah.
2
Examine the proofs for each view
For each position, cite the key hadith and explain how it is interpreted. The Hanafi proof uses the hadith 'Allah has added for you a prayer...' as an imperative; the opposing side uses the hadith 'Three things are written upon me...' and the hadith of the Bedouin.
3
Analyze the number of rak'ahs
Why does the Hanafi school insist on three rak'ahs with one taslim? Compare with the Shafi'i flexibility of 1-11 rak'ahs. The Hanafi proof includes practice of the Prophet and a claim of consensus.
4
Understand the qunut dispute
Where and when is qunut recited? Hanafi: in the third rak'ah before ruku' all year. Shafi'i: after ruku' in the last half of Ramadan. Know the proof from Ubayy ibn Ka'b and the interpretation of 'qunut'.
5
Derive the practical consequences
How does the dispute affect daily practice? For example, if one remembers Witr during Fajr, Abu Hanifa says Fajr is invalid; Shafi'i says no. This shows the thamarah al-khilaf.

باب الوتر والنوافل في بيان أحكام الوتر ك ف و ك ف ط و الوتر واجب، وهو ثلاث ركعات بتسليمةٍ، وقنت في ثالثته قبل الركوع أبداً وهي أي الوتر المصلي . في جميع السنة باب الوتر والنوافل: لما فرغ من بيان الفرائض وآدابها وفضائلها شرع في بيان النوافل، وأخرها؛ لأنها مكملات ومتممات، وجمع بين الوتر والنوافل؛ لأنه كالنفل عندهما وعند الشافعي، والوتر – بفتح الواو وكسرها – خلاف الشفع والنوافل جمع نافلة وهي في اللغة الزيادة وفي الشرع عبارة عن قربة زائدة على الفرائض والواجبات والسنن. (مسكين، فتح) الوتر واجب أي اعتقاداً حتى لا يكفر جاحده، وفرض عملاً حتى لو تذكره في الفجر فسد فرض الفجر عند الإمام، وسنة سبباً، فإن ثبوته بالسنة وقالا والشافعي هو أكد من سائر السنن، واحتجوا بما روي عن النبي عليه أنه قال: "ثلاث كتب علي، ولم يكتب عليكم الوتر والضحى والأضحية، وفي رواية: "كتب علي، وهي لكم سنة"؛ ولحديث الأعرابي: هل على غيرهن؟ فقال: "لا إلا أن تطوع، وللإمام قوله عليه: "إن الله زاد لكم صلاة، الوتر، فصلوها ما بين العشاء إلى طلوع الفجر، والاستدلال به من وجهين: أحدهما: أنه أمر والأمر للوجوب، والثاني: أنه سمّي زيادةً، والزيادة لا يكون إلا من جنسه، وإذا كان من غير جنسه يكون قراناً لا زيادةً، والزيادة إنما يتصور على المقدر وهو الفرض؛ إذ النوافل غير منحصرة. (مستخلص) ألا! وهي ثلاث ركعات: أي كصلاة المغرب في الأوقات كلها، وقال الشافعي: هو بالخيار إن شاء أوتر بركعة أو بثلاث أو بخمس إلى إحدى عشرة ولا يزيد على هذا في الأوقات كلها، وقال الزهري في شهر رمضان ثلاث ركعات وفي غيره ركعة للشافعي قوله عليه: "من شاء أوتر بركعة، ومن شاء أوتر بثلاث ركعات أو خمس"، ولنا: ما روي عن ابن مسعود وابن عباس وعائشة رضي الله عنهم أنهم قالوا: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يوتر بثلاث ركعات، وعن الحسن أنه قال: "أجمع المسلمون على أن الوتر ثلاث ركعات، لا سلام إلا في آخرهن". وحديث التخيير محمول على ما قبل استقرار الوتر. (مستخلص) بتسليمة واحدة كالمغرب حتى لو اقتدى فيه من يسلم على رأس الركعتين وسلم فسد اقتداؤه على الأصح. (ط،ع) قبل الركوع أبداً: لما روي عن أبي بن كعب رضي الله عنه أنه كان يوتر بثلاث ركعات يقرأ في الأولى بـ سَبِّحِ اسْمَ رَبِّكَ الْأَعْلَى وفي الثانية بـ ﴿قُلْ يَا أَيُّهَا الْكَافِرُونَ﴾ وفي الثالثة بـ ﴿قُلْ هُوَ اللَّهُ أَحَدٌ﴾. ويقنت قبل الركوع، وعند الشافعي يقنت بعد الركوع، وقوله: "أبداً" احتراز عن قوله: "أيضاً"، فإنه لا قنوت عنده إلا في النصف الأخير من رمضان، واحتج بما روي أن النبي لما أمر أبي بن كعب بالإمامة في ليالي رمضان أمره بالقنوت في النصف الأخير منه، وتأويل ما رواه طول القيام والقنوت في اللغة الطاعة والقيام والدعاء، ودعاء القنوت في الوتر: "اللهم إنا نستعينك ونستغفرك إلخ" عند الحنفية، أو "اللهم اهدني في من هديت" كما يقرأه أصحاب الشافعي في الفجر، عمر.
Chapter of Witr and Nawafil: On explaining the rulings of Witr. Witr is wajib, and it is three rak'ahs with one taslim, and he makes qunut in its third before ruku' always. And it [i.e., the prayer] is by the one who prays in all the year. Chapter of Witr and Nawafil: When he finished explaining the obligations (fara'id) and their manners and virtues, he began to explain the supererogatory prayers (nawafil), and delayed them because they are completers and perfecters. And he combined Witr and Nawafil because according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, it is like nafl. And 'al-witr' with fatha of waw and kasra is the opposite of shaf' (even). Nawafil is the plural of nafila, which lexically means increase, and in the religious law it is an expression for a supererogatory act of worship additional to the fara'id, wajibat, and sunan. (Miskin, Fath) Witr is wajib: i.e., in belief, so that one who denies it is not declared an unbeliever, and practically obligatory (fard 'amalan) such that if he remembers it during Fajr, his Fajr prayer is invalidated according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And it is sunnah in terms of its proof, for its establishment is by the Sunnah. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i said it is more emphasized than other sunan. They (the Sahibayn and Shafi'i) argued with what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Three things are written upon me but not upon you: Witr, Duha, and Udhiya (sacrifice).' And in another narration: 'Written upon me, and they are a sunnah for you.' And the hadith of the Bedouin: 'Are there any besides them?' He said: 'No, unless you do voluntarily.' And the Imam (Abu Hanifa) used his statement: 'Indeed Allah has added for you a prayer, al-Witr, so pray it between Isha and dawn.' And the deduction from this is from two aspects: one, it is a command and the command indicates obligation; two, He called it an addition, and an addition can only be of the same kind, and if it were from a different kind it would be a pairing (qiran) not an increase, and increase is only conceivable upon what is predetermined, which is the fard, because nawafil are unlimited. (Mustakhlas) Know that they are three rak'ahs: i.e., like Maghrib in all seasons. Al-Shafi'i said: He has a choice: if he wishes he may pray one rak'ah, or three, or up to eleven, and he may not exceed this in all seasons. And al-Zuhri said: In Ramadan three rak'ahs, and in other times one rak'ah. For al-Shafi'i is his statement: 'Whoever wishes may pray one rak'ah, whoever wishes may pray three or five.' And our proof is what was narrated from Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Abbas, and 'A'isha that they said: 'The Messenger of Allah used to pray Witr with three rak'ahs.' And from al-Hasan that he said: 'The Muslims have unanimously agreed that Witr is three rak'ahs, with no taslim except at the end of them.' And the hadith of choice is interpreted as before the establishment of Witr. (Mustakhlas) With one taslim like Maghrib, so much so that if someone follows him and gives taslim after two rak'ahs, his following is invalid according to the most correct view. (T, 'Ayni) Before ruku' always: based on what was narrated from Ubayy ibn Ka'b that he used to pray Witr with three rak'ahs, reciting in the first 'Sabbih isma rabbika al-a'la', in the second 'Qul ya ayyuha al-kafirun', and in the third 'Qul huwa Allahu ahad', and he made qunut before ruku'. According to al-Shafi'i, he makes qunut after ruku'. And his statement 'always' is a precaution against his statement 'also' (aydan), for he (al-Shafi'i) does not have qunut except in the last half of Ramadan. He (al-Shafi'i) argued with what was narrated that when the Prophet ordered Ubayy ibn Ka'b to lead the prayers in the nights of Ramadan, he ordered him to make qunut in the last half of it. And the interpretation of what was narrated is that 'qunut' means prolonged standing, and qunut in language means obedience, standing, and supplication. And the supplication of qunut in Witr according to Hanafis is: 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka wa nastaghfiruka...' (O Allah, we seek Your help and ask Your forgiveness...), or according to Shafi'is in Fajr: 'Allahumma ihdini fi man hadayta...' (O Allah, guide me among those You have guided...).
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on Witr and Nawafil (supererogatory prayers). The author first explains the connection: after discussing fard prayers, he now discusses supererogatory acts, which complete the obligations. Witr is included because according to the two main disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i, it is considered a nafl (supererogatory) prayer, though Imam Abu Hanifa holds it is wajib (obligatory). The lexicography of 'witr' and 'nafl' is given. The core ruling: Witr is wajib according to Abu Hanifa. He explains that wajib here means in belief (i'tiqadan) such that the denier is not declared a kafir, and it is practically obligatory ('amalan) such that if one remembers he has not prayed Witr during Fajr prayer, his Fajr is invalidated (according to Abu Hanifa) because it resembles a fard in terms of temporal obligation. However, its proof is from the Sunnah, so it is sunnah in terms of source (sababan). The Sahibayn and Shafi'i consider it a highly emphasized sunnah. The proof cited includes hadith where the Prophet said three things are obligatory upon him but not upon the community, and the hadith of the Bedouin, and the hadith 'Allah has added for you a prayer, al-Witr.' Abu Hanifa argues that this command indicates obligation (wujub) and the term 'increase' implies it is like the fard prayers because increase can only be of the same type. The Sahibayn and Shafi'i use the hadith 'three things written upon me' to show it is not obligatory for others. Then the passage discusses the number of rak'ahs: the Hanafi position is three rak'ahs with one taslim (like Maghrib). Shafi'i allows choice from one to eleven. The Hanafi proof is from the practice of the Prophet as reported by Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Abbas, and Aisha, and a claim of consensus (ijma') by Hasan al-Basri that Witr is three rak'ahs. The hadith giving choice is interpreted as before the ruling was settled. Then regarding qunut: Hanafis do qunut before ruku' in the third rak'ah throughout the year. Shafi'is do qunut after ruku' and only in the last half of Ramadan. The Hanafi proof is from the practice of Ubayy ibn Ka'b. The term 'qunut' originally means standing or obedience, but Shafi'is consider it a specific supplication. Hanafis recite 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka...' while Shafi'is have a different supplication for Fajr.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): A prayer performed after Isha, comprising an odd number of rak'ahs, in the Hanafi school specifically three rak'ahs with one taslim.
القنوت (al-qunut): A supplication recited in the third rak'ah of Witr after recitation but before ruku', according to Hanafis.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Legal obligation, distinct from fard in Hanafi terminology; wajib is proven by zanni evidence, fard by qat'i evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens if a person remembers during Fajr prayer that he has not yet prayed Witr?
A) He should continue Fajr and pray Witr after
B) His Fajr prayer is invalid because Witr becomes a fard 'amalan
C) He must stop Fajr and pray Witr immediately
D) Nothing; Witr is not obligatory

Key Takeaway
Witr is a wajib prayer in the Hanafi school, consisting of three rak'ahs with one taslim and qunut before ruku' throughout the year, based on a command and consensus of the early Muslims.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

The Hanafi Position on Witr: Wajib, Three Rak‘ahs, Qunut Before Ruku‘
Defining the legal status and characteristics of witr prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds witr as wajib, identify the key evidence for three rak‘ahs, and distinguish the Hanafi qunut placement from the Shafi‘i view.

Core Idea
Witr is wajib, not sunnah—its proof is a command from Allah, yet it is not fard because the evidence is zanni.

Witr prayer occupies a unique position in Hanafi fiqh: it is not a fard, but it is more than a sunnah. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind the Hanafi claim that witr is wajib, the insistence on three rak‘ahs, and the placement of qunut before ruku‘. You will encounter the main evidence and the inter-madhhab dispute, particularly with the Shafi‘i school, and see how the Hanafis defend their view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim on witr's legal status
Note the text states 'al-witr wajib' and explains three dimensions: in belief (denier not a disbeliever), in practice (if remembered during Fajr, Fajr is invalid), and in cause (proven by Sunnah).
This distinguishes wajib from fard (where denial is disbelief) and from sunnah (where no invalidation of Fajr occurs).
2
Examine the evidence for wajib
The key dalil is the Prophet's statement: 'Allah has added a prayer for you: witr, so pray it.' The Hanafi istidlal: (1) the command 'pray it' indicates obligation (amr yufid al-wujub); (2) the term 'addition' implies witr is of the same genus as fard, thus obligatory.
The other hadith about 'three things prescribed upon me' is used by the opposition (Sahibayn and Shafi‘i) to argue sunnah.
3
Analyze the dispute on number of rak‘ahs
Hanafis insist on three rak‘ahs with one taslim like Maghrib. Shafi‘i allows 1, 3, 5, up to 11. Hanafi evidence: consensus of Companions (Ibn Mas‘ud, Ibn Abbas, Aisha) that the Prophet always prayed three, and the hadith of choice is abrogated or refers to before the ruling was settled.
The consequence: a Hanafi cannot pray witr as one rak‘ah; doing so would be invalid.
4
Locate the qunut dispute
Hanafis hold qunut before ruku‘ in the third rak‘ah always. Shafi‘is hold qunut after ruku‘ and only in the last half of Ramadan. Hanafi proof: the practice of the Prophet reported by Ubayy ibn Ka‘b and the general practice of the Companions.
The term 'abadan' (always) in the matn excludes the Shafi‘i view of limiting qunut to certain nights.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically: for Hanafis, missing witr is a sin (though not disbelief); for Shafi‘is, missing it is leaving a sunnah, no sin. Only three rak‘ahs are valid in Hanafi; Shafi‘is have flexibility. Qunut before ruku‘ vs after changes the prayer's form.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Imam Abu Hanifa's view (wajib, three rak‘ahs, qunut before ruku‘).

Chapter of Witr and Supererogatory Prayers. In explanation of the rulings of witr. The witr is obligatory, and it is three rak'ahs with one taslim, and qunut in its third before bowing always. The chapter: When he finished explaining the obligatory acts and their etiquettes and virtues, he began explaining the supererogatory acts, and he delayed them because they are completions and perfections. He combined witr and supererogatory prayers because it is like supererogatory according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i. Al-Witr (with fatha or kasra on the waw) is the opposite of shaf' (even). Nawafil is plural of nafila, which linguistically means increase, and in Sacred Law it is an expression for an act of devotion additional to the obligatory acts, duties, and sunnas. (Miskeen, Fath) Al-Witr is obligatory, i.e., in belief such that its denier does not become a disbeliever, and it is a fard in practice such that if he remembers it during Fajr, the Fajr fard is invalid according to Imam, and it is a sunnah in cause because its proof is from the Sunnah. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i said: it is the most emphasized of the sunan. They adduced what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: "Three things are prescribed upon me but not upon you: witr, Duha, and Udhiya." And in a narration: "Prescribed upon me, and for you they are sunnah." And the hadith of the Bedouin: "Are there other than them?" He said: "No, unless you volunteer." And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is his (peace be upon him) statement: "Indeed Allah has added a prayer for you: witr, so pray it between Isha and the break of dawn." And the inference from it is in two aspects: one: that it is a command, and a command indicates obligation; the second: that he called it an addition, and addition can only be of the same genus, and if it were of a different genus it would be a conjunction not an addition, and addition can only be conceived upon that which is measured (i.e., the fard); since supererogatory acts are not limited. (Mustakhlas) Indeed! And it is three rak'ahs: i.e., like the Maghrib prayer in all times. Al-Shafi'i said: He has a choice: if he wishes he may pray witr as one rak'ah, or three, or five up to eleven, and he does not exceed that in all times. And al-Zuhri said: In Ramadan three rak'ahs, and in other than it, one rak'ah. For al-Shafi'i is his (peace be upon him) statement: "Whoever wishes may pray witr as one rak'ah, and whoever wishes may pray witr as three or five." Our evidence is what was narrated from Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, and Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them) that they said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to pray witr as three rak'ahs. And from al-Hasan that he said: "The Muslims have agreed that witr is three rak'ahs, with no salutation except at the end of them." The hadith of choice is interpreted as referring to before the witr was settled. (Mustakhlas) With one taslim like Maghrib, such that if someone praying behind him makes taslim at the end of two rak'ahs and says salam, his following is invalid according to the soundest opinion. (T, A) Before bowing always: Because it was narrated from Ubayy ibn Ka'b that he (peace be upon him) used to pray witr as three rak'ahs, reciting in the first "Glorify the Name of your Lord, the Most High," in the second "Say: O disbelievers," and in the third "Say: He is Allah, the One." And he would perform qunut before bowing. According to al-Shafi'i, he performs qunut after bowing. His (the author's) statement "always" guards against his statement "also", for according to him (al-Shafi'i?) there is no qunut except in the last half of Ramadan. He adduced what was narrated that when 'Umar appointed Ubayy ibn Ka'b as imam during the nights of Ramadan, he ordered him to perform qunut in the last half of it. The interpretation of what he narrated is the length of standing. Qunut linguistically means obedience, standing, and supplication. The supplication of qunut in witr: "O Allah, we seek Your help and ask Your forgiveness etc." according to the Hanafis, or "O Allah, guide me among those You have guided" as the followers of al-Shafi'i recite in Fajr.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the status and form of the witr prayer. The author begins by stating that witr is wajib (obligatory), consisting of three rak'ahs with a single taslim, and that qunut is recited in the third rak'ah before ruku' at all times. The commentary then elaborates: the chapter on witr and nawafil is placed after discussing obligatory prayers because nawafil are complementary. The term 'witr' is defined linguistically and legally. The Hanafi position is that witr is wajib in the sense that denying it does not render one a disbeliever (as it would for fard), but it is fard in practice such that if one remembers it during Fajr prayer, the Fajr prayer becomes invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa. However, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i consider it the most emphasized sunnah. The text then provides proofs: the hadith stating three things are prescribed for the Prophet but not for the Ummah, and the hadith 'Allah has added a prayer for you: witr'. The author argues that the command indicates obligation. Regarding the number of rak'ahs, the Hanafis insist on three, citing consensus of the Companions and rejection of the Shafi'i view allowing 1, 3, 5, etc. The hadith of choice is interpreted as abrogated. Finally, the placement of qunut before ruku' is defended, with the Shafi'i view of after ruku' rejected through the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Companions. The qunut invocation is specified as the Hanafi version.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after Isha, consisting of three rak'ahs in the Hanafi school.
القنوت (al-qunut): A supplication recited while standing, specifically in the witr prayer before ruku'.
النافلة (al-nafilah): Supererogatory act of worship; in this context, prayers additional to the fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal status of the witr prayer?
A) Fard (obligatory, denial is disbelief)
B) Wajib (obligatory, but denial is not disbelief)
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized sunnah)
D) Mustahabb (recommended)

Key Takeaway
Witr is wajib in the Hanafi school: three rak‘ahs with one taslim and qunut before ruku‘, supported by the command in the hadith and the practice of the Companions.

Salah — Prayer

Rulings on Qunut and Following the Imam in Witr
Explaining rules of qunut and follower's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on qunut in witr, justify the abrogation of qunut in Fajr, and apply the conditions for a Hanafi follower to pray behind a Shafi'i imam.

Core Idea
Qunut in Fajr was abrogated; the follower's prayer depends on his own belief, not the imam's.

Qunut is a supplication recited in prayer, but where and when it is performed varies between schools. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the Hanafi position: qunut is only in witr, before ruku'. It also addresses the follower's role and whether a Hanafi can follow a Shafi'i imam who performs qunut differently. The key principle is that the prayer is unified—differences in belief do not inherently invalidate following. However, the imam must avoid actions that would nullify the prayer in the follower's school. Let's unravel these rulings step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling for qunut
The passage states that qunut is performed only in witr, not in other prayers. According to the Hanafi school, it is a sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah), not wajib.
The ruling applies specifically to the supplication itself; witr is wajib in Hanafi, but qunut is sunnah.
2
Follow the proof for abrogation of qunut in Fajr
The text cites a hadith: the Prophet performed qunut in Fajr for a month supplicating against specific tribes, then abandoned it. This abandonment indicates abrogation (naskh), so qunut in Fajr is not part of regular prayer.
The Hanafi school accepts naskh of sunnah by sunnah; the proof is from the Prophet's own practice.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the follower's action
There is a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the follower should follow the imam in reciting qunut, while Muhammad holds that the follower should only say amin. The mufta bihi is the former.
The commentary favors the first view (الأول أظهر).
4
Understand the inter-madhhab dispute on following a Shafi'i imam
The Shafi'i school performs qunut in Fajr and after ruku' in witr. The Hanafi school allows following a Shafi'i imam under conditions: if the imam is known to avoid areas of disagreement (e.g., renews wudu after cupping), following is permissible without dislike. If he is known not to avoid them, it is impermissible. If unknown, it is permissible with dislike.
The principle: the validity of the follower's prayer depends on the follower's own belief, not the imam's.
5
Extract the key principle: follower's belief governs
The passage concludes that the valid opinion considers the follower's belief. As long as the imam does not perform an action that would invalidate the prayer in the follower's school, the prayer is valid.
This is a practical maxim for Hanafis praying behind non-Hanafi imams.

Source Text

(مسكين فتح قانت أي الإمام القانت في قنوته ويخفي هو والقوم؛ لأنه دعاء.(ع) لا الفجر: أي لا يتبع قانت الفجر بل يسكت من خلفه عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يتبعه، ثم قيل: يقف قائماً ولا يقنت، وقيل: يقعد، والأول أظهر، ودلت المسألة على جواز الاقتداء بشافعي المذهب، وجه الدلالة أنه الاقتداء به لم يصلح اختلاف علمائنا في أنه يسكت أو يتابعه، وما قيل من فساد اقتداء الحنفي بالشافعي في الوتر وصلاة العيدين - محتجا بأنه اقتداء المفترض بالمتنفل؛ إذ الحنفي يعتقد وجوبها والشافعي سنيتها - وجه صحة الاقتداء هو أن الصلاة متحدة لا تختلف باختلاف الاعتقاد وأحسن ما قيل في الاقتداء بالشافعي إنه إن علم منه أنه يتوقى في مواضع الخلاف مثلاً يجدد وضوؤه من الحجامة والفصد، ويغسل ثوبة من المني ونحوها جاز الاقتداء به بلا كراهة وإن علم أنه لا يتوقاها لم يجز الاقتداء به، وإن جهل حاله جاز الاقتداء مع الكراهة والجامع لهذه الأقوال أن لا يتحقق منه ما يفسد صلاته في اعتقاده؛ بناء على أن المعتبر رأي المقتدي، وهو الصحيح وعليه الأكثر (مسكين، فتح)
After he says the takbir, and recites in each rak'ah of it the Fatiha and a surah, and he does not perform qunut for other than it (witr), and the follower follows, raising his hands... The one performing qunut in witr, not in Fajr. And the sunnah before Fajr equals the fard of Fajr. Meaning: for other than witr, the follower. And it is preferred to combine both (i.e., the dua and the reported formula). And whoever does not memorize the reported supplication recites: 'Our Lord, give us in this world etc.' And it is said: he says: 'O Allah, forgive me' three times. And should he recite aloud or softly? All are permissible, and the chosen is the second. (Mustakhlas, Fath) And a surah with it or three verses is obligatory. (T) meaning any surah he wishes, but what is narrated from him (peace be upon him) is that he recited in the first rak'ah 'Surah al-A'la', in the second 'Surah al-Kafirun', and in the third 'Surah al-Ikhlas'. If he recites that, it is good for following. (Fath, Ayni) And he does not perform qunut for other than it (witr): What is narrated of his (peace be upon him) performing qunut in Fajr was only for a month supplicating against a group of Arabs, then he abandoned it, as narrated by a group of Companions including Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him): the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed qunut in Fajr prayer for a month, supplicating against [the tribes of] Ri'l, Dhakwan, and 'Usayya, saying: 'O Allah, make Your punishment severe on Mudar, and make upon them years like the years of Yusuf and Yunus,' then he abandoned it, so it was abrogated. (Fath, Mustakhlas) And the follower follows: i.e., the follower follows the imam in reciting the qunut supplication in witr. According to Muhammad: he does not follow but rather says amin. And it is said: he remains silent. Al-Tahawi mentioned that the people follow him up to his saying 'mulhaq'; then when he supplicates, according to Abu Yusuf they follow him, and according to Muhammad they say amin. (Miskeen, Fath) Qanat (the one performing qunut) means the imam who is performing qunut in his qunut, and he and the people recite silently because it is a supplication. (A) Not in Fajr: i.e., he does not follow the one performing qunut in Fajr, rather those behind him remain silent according to both sides. Abu Yusuf said: he follows him. Then it was said: he stands and does not perform qunut. And it was said: he sits. The first is more apparent. And this issue indicates the validity of following a Shafi'i imam. The evidence: following him is not invalidated by the difference of our scholars regarding whether he remains silent or follows him. And what is said about the invalidity of a Hanafi following a Shafi'i in witr and Eid prayers – arguing that it is the following of one who considers it fard by one who considers it sunnah – the validity of following is that the prayer is one and does not differ due to difference in belief. And the best of what is said about following a Shafi'i is: if it is known that he avoids areas of disagreement, e.g., he renews his ablution from cupping and phlebotomy, and washes his garment from semen and the like, then following him is permissible without dislike. If it is known that he does not avoid them, then following him is not permissible. If his state is unknown, then following him is permissible with dislike. The summary of these statements is that what would invalidate his prayer in his own belief should not be confirmed from him, based on the principle that the valid opinion is that of the follower, and this is correct and the majority hold it. (Miskeen, Fath)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the recitation of qunut and the rules for the follower. The author explains the procedure after the takbir in witr: reciting Fatiha and a surah in each rak'ah, that qunut is only performed in witr, and that the follower follows the imam in the qunut supplication. The text then clarifies that qunut is not performed in other prayers, specifically not in Fajr, as the Prophet's qunut in Fajr was a temporary act of supplication against specific tribes and was later abrogated. The ruling that the follower should follow the imam in qunut is then examined: according to Imam Muhammad, the follower should say amin rather than recite the supplication; Abu Yusuf holds that they should follow the imam. The author also discusses the permissibility of a Hanafi praying behind a Shafi'i imam. The Shafi'i imam considers witr a sunnah, while the Hanafi considers it wajib. This difference in belief does not invalidate the prayer because the act of prayer itself is unified. However, if the Shafi'i imam is known to neglect areas of disagreement (e.g., not renewing wudu after cupping), then following him is disliked or impermissible. If his practice is unknown, it is permissible with dislike. The underlying principle is that the validity of the follower's prayer depends on his own belief, not the imam's.

Key Terms
القنوت (al-qunut): The supplication recited in the witr prayer, and according to Hanafis, it is only in witr before ruku'.
المؤتم (al-mu'tamm): The follower in congregational prayer, who follows the imam.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later revelation or practice; here, the qunut in Fajr was abrogated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Qunut in Fajr is abrogated; the follower's prayer depends on his own belief, and following a Shafi'i imam is permissible if the imam avoids points of khilaf.

The Sunnah Before Fajr and Ranking of Other Sunnah Prayers
Explaining the emphasis on the two rak'ahs before Fajr and ranking of sunan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ranking of the regular sunnah prayers and identify the most emphasized sunnah according to the school.

Core Idea
The two rak'ahs before Fajr are the strongest sunnah; the sunnah before Zuhr is also highly emphasized.

This lesson explores the Hanafi classification of the sunan al-ratibah, the regular sunnah prayers associated with the five daily prayers. The source text presents the hierarchy established by Al-Halawani and a correction from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, highlighting the unique emphasis on the Fajr sunnah. We will examine the ranking and the evidence behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
Recognize that the two rak'ahs before Fajr are the most strongly emphasized sunnah prayer.
This is unanimous in the Hanafi school.
2
List the order of emphasis
Note the sequence: Fajr, then Maghrib, then after Zuhr, then before Zuhr (disputed), then after Isha.
Al-Halawani's ranking is given, followed by a correction from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
3
Recognize the differences in scholarly opinion
Identify the dispute regarding the sunnah before Zuhr and the alternative view that all sunan are equal.
The hadith about the four rak'ahs before Zuhr gives it additional emphasis.
4
Apply the hadith evidence
Understand the hadith: 'Whoever leaves the four (rak'ahs) before Zuhr will not attain my intercession.'
This hadith elevates the sunnah before Zuhr above the others according to the correction.
5
Understand the extreme emphasis on Fajr sunnah
Note that denying the sunnah of Fajr is considered a serious matter, to the point of disbelief (in the sense of rejecting a well-established sunnah).
This underscores its unique status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسنة قبل الفجر إلخ: ذكر الحلواني أن أقوى السنن ركعتا الفجر ثم سنة المغرب؛ فإنه عليه لم يدعهما في سفر ولا حضر، ثم التي بعد الظهر، فإنها متفق عليها والتي قبلها مختلف فيها، وقيل: هي للفصل بين الأذان والإقامة، ثم التي بعد العشاء، ثم التي قبل الظهر. ونقل في البحر [٩٠/٢] تصحيح أن التي قبل الظهر أكد بعد ركعتي الفجر معللاً بأنه ورد فيها وعيد، وهو قوله عليه من ترك الأربع قبل الظهر لم تنله شفاعتي ، وقيل : الأربع قبل الظهر والركعتان بعده وبعد المغرب والعشاء كلها سواء، وسنة الفجر أقوى لأنه يكفر جاحد سنيتها.(فتح)
And the sunnah before Fajr etc.: Al-Halawani mentioned that the strongest of the sunan are the two rak'ahs of Fajr, then the sunnah of Maghrib, because he (peace be upon him) did not leave them in travel or residence. Then those after Zuhr, for they are agreed upon, while those before it are disputed. It is said they are for the separation between the adhan and iqama. Then those after Isha, then those before Zuhr. It is transmitted in al-Bahr [90/2] the correction that the (sunnah) before Zuhr is more emphasized after the two rak'ahs of Fajr, because a threat has been reported concerning it, namely his (peace be upon him) statement: "Whoever leaves the four (rak'ahs) before Zuhr will not attain my intercession." And it is said: the four before Zuhr, the two after it, and after Maghrib and Isha are all equal. The sunnah of Fajr is strongest because its denier commits disbelief (in the sense of it being a sunnah). (Fath)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the ranking of sunnah prayers. It begins by noting that the two rak'ahs before Fajr (sunnah al-Fajr) are the strongest of the sunan, followed by the sunnah of Maghrib, as the Prophet never abandoned them in travel or residence. Next is the sunnah after Zuhr, which is agreed upon, while the sunnah before Zuhr is subject to dispute. According to one view, it is intended to separate the adhan and iqama. Then come the sunnah after Isha and before Zuhr. However, a correction from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states that the sunnah before Zuhr is more emphasized after the Fajr sunnah, due to a hadith threatening that the one who abandons the four rak'ahs before Zuhr will not receive the Prophet's intercession. Another view holds that all these sunan (four before Zuhr, two after, two after Maghrib, two after Isha) are equal. The passage concludes that the sunnah of Fajr is the strongest because its denier is considered to reject a well-established sunnah, which could lead to disbelief (though this is a severe deterrent, not actual disbelief).

Key Terms
السنة الفجر (al-sunnah al-fajr): The two rak'ahs of sunnah prayer before the Fajr fard prayer.
السنن الراتبة (al-sunan al-ratibah): The regular sunnah prayers associated with the five daily prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The two rak'ahs before Fajr
B) The four rak'ahs before Zuhr
C) The two rak'ahs after Maghrib
D) The two rak'ahs after Isha

Key Takeaway
The two rak'ahs before Fajr are the most emphasized sunnah prayer in the Hanafi school, followed by the sunnah after Maghrib and the sunnah after Zuhr.

Qunut in Witr and Following a Shafi'i Imam
Rulings on Qunut in Witr and following the imam, including permissibility of following a Shafi'i imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Qunut in Witr and evaluate the permissibility of following a Shafi'i imam in Witr prayer.

Core Idea
Qunut is for Witr, not Fajr; following a Shafi'i imam is permissible if no invalidation occurs.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on Qunut in Witr and the complex issue of following an imam from a different madhhab. The text addresses both internal Hanafi differences on the follower's role during Qunut and the broader inter-madhhab question of praying behind a Shafi'i imam who considers Witr sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
Recognize that the Hanafi school assigns Qunut exclusively to Witr prayer, not to Fajr. The text opens with 'Qānīt al-witr lā al-fajr'.
2
Understand the evidence for abrogation
The Prophet (peace be upon him) performed Qunut in Fajr for only one month, praying against specific tribes, then abandoned it. This abrogation is cited to justify the Hanafi position.
3
Examine the internal dispute on the follower's action
The text presents three views: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the muqtadi follows the imam in reciting Qunut; Muhammad says he says 'Ameen'; another view says he remains silent.
This internal ikhtilaf does not invalidate the prayer.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab issue
The key challenge: a Hanafi believes Witr is wajib, while a Shafi'i considers it sunnah. The text argues the prayer remains valid because the act of prayer is one and does not vary with belief.
5
Apply the condition for following a Shafi'i imam
The follower must ascertain that the imam avoids actions that would invalidate the prayer according to the Hanafi school (e.g., breaking wudu). The ruling varies based on knowledge of the imam's caution.
The mufta bihi: consideration is the follower's belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قانت الوتر لا الفجر. والأحب الجمع بينهما، ومن لا يحفظ الدعاء المأثورة يقرأ: "ربنا آتنا في الدنيا إلخ"، وقيل: يقول: "اللهم اغفر لي" ثلاثاً، وهل يقرأ جهراً أو إخفاء؟ الكل واسع، والمختار هو الثاني. وسورة معها أو ثلاث آيات وجوباً. أي أي سورة شاء، ولكن المروي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام: أنه قرأ في الركعة الأولى "سورة الأعلى" وفي الثانية "الكافرون" وفي الثالثة "سورة الإخلاص"، فإن قرأها كان حسناً للاتباع. ولا يقنت لغيره: وما روي من قنوته عليه الصلاة والسلام في الفجر، فإنما كان شهراً يدعو على قوم من العرب، ثم تركه كما روي عن جماعة الصحابة منهم ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه: أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قنت في صلاة الفجر شهراً، وكان يدعو على رعل وذكوان وعُصَيَّة، ويقول: "اللهم اشدد وطأتك على مضر، واجعل عليهم سنين كسني يوسف ويونس"، ثم تركه فكان منسوخاً. ويتبع المؤتم: أي يتبع المقتدي الإمام في قراءة دعاء القنوت في الوتر، وعند محمد: لا يتبع بل يؤمن، وقيل: يسكت، وذكر الطحاوي أن القوم يتابعونه إلى قوله: "ملحق"، فإذا دعا فعند أبي يوسف يتابعونه، وعند محمد: يؤمنون. قانت أي الإمام القانت في قنوته ويخفي هو والقوم؛ لأنه دعاء. لا الفجر: أي لا يتبع قانت الفجر بل يسكت من خلفه عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يتبعه، ثم قيل: يقف قائماً ولا يقنت، وقيل: يقعد، والأول أظهر، ودلت المسألة على جواز الاقتداء بشافعي المذهب، وجه الدلالة أنه الاقتداء به لم يصلح اختلاف علمائنا في أنه يسكت أو يتابعه، وما قيل من فساد اقتداء الحنفي بالشافعي في الوتر وصلاة العيدين - محتجا بأنه اقتداء المفترض بالمتنفل؛ إذ الحنفي يعتقد وجوبها والشافعي سنيتها - وجه صحة الاقتداء هو أن الصلاة متحدة لا تختلف باختلاف الاعتقاد وأحسن ما قيل في الاقتداء بالشافعي إنه إن علم منه أنه يتوقى في مواضع الخلاف مثلاً يجدد وضوؤه من الحجامة والفصد، ويغسل ثوبه من المني ونحوها جاز الاقتداء به بلا كراهة وإن علم أنه لا يتوقاها لم يجز الاقتداء به، وإن جهل حاله جاز الاقتداء مع الكراهة والجامع لهذه الأقوال أن لا يتحقق منه ما يفسد صلاته في اعتقاده؛ بناء على أن المعتبر رأي المقتدي، وهو الصحيح وعليه الأكثر
The reciter of Qunut is for Witr, not for Fajr. It is preferable to combine both (du'as). Whoever does not remember the transmitted du'a recites: 'Our Lord, give us in the world, etc.' It is said he says: 'O Allah, forgive me' three times. Should he recite aloud or softly? All are permissible, but the chosen is the second. And a Surah with it or three verses obligatorily. Meaning any surah he wishes, but what is narrated from him (peace be upon him) is that he recited in the first rak'ah 'Surah al-A'la', in the second 'Surah al-Kafirun', and in the third 'Surah al-Ikhlas'; if he recites that, it is good due to following. And he does not recite Qunut for other than it (Witr). What is narrated of his Qunut in Fajr was only for a month praying against a group of Arabs, then he abandoned it as narrated from a group of Companions including Ibn Mas'ud: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) recited Qunut in Fajr for a month, praying against Ri'l, Dhakwan, and 'Usayyah, saying: 'O Allah, make your punishment severe on Mudar, and make upon them years like the years of Yusuf and Yunus,' then he abandoned it, so it was abrogated. And the follower follows the imam: meaning the muqtadi follows the imam in reciting the Qunut supplication in Witr. According to Muhammad: he does not follow but says Ameen, and it is said he remains silent. Al-Tahawi mentioned that the people follow him up to his saying 'Mulaqq', then when he supplicates, according to Abu Yusuf they follow him, and according to Muhammad they say Ameen. 'Qanit' meaning the imam who is reciting Qunut, and he and the people recite quietly because it is a supplication. 'Not for Fajr': meaning the Qunut of Fajr is not followed; rather, those behind remain silent according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: he follows him. Then it is said: he stands without reciting Qunut, and it is said: he sits, and the first is more apparent. This issue indicates the permissibility of following a Shafi'i imam. The evidence is that following him is not invalidated by the difference of our scholars regarding whether he remains silent or follows him. What is said about the invalidity of a Hanafi following a Shafi'i in Witr and Eid prayers - arguing that it is a muqtadi (obligated) following a mutanawwil (supererogatory) because the Hanafi believes them obligatory and the Shafi'i sunnah - the validity of following is that the prayer is one and does not differ with difference of belief. The best said about following a Shafi'i is that if one knows he is cautious in points of difference, e.g., he renews his wudu from cupping and phlebotomy, washes his garment from semen and the like, then following him is permissible without dislike. If one knows he is not cautious, it is not permissible. If his state is unknown, following is permissible with dislike. The synthesizer of these sayings is that nothing that invalidates his prayer in his (the follower's) belief is ascertained from him; based on that the consideration is the opinion of the follower, and that is correct and upon it is the majority.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and internal Hanafi dispute, also touching on inter-madhhab issues. It begins with the rule: Qunut is recited in Witr, not in Fajr. The imam recites Qunut aloud? Actually softly because it is a supplication. The muqtadi follows the imam in reciting the Qunut? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the muqtadi follows the imam in reciting the Qunut du'a. Imam Muhammad says the muqtadi should not recite but say Ameen. Another view is to remain silent. The passage then addresses the issue of following a Shafi'i imam in Witr, where the Shafi'i considers Witr sunnah while the Hanafi considers it wajib. The Hanafi position allows following a Shafi'i imam, provided the imam does not do anything that would invalidate the prayer according to the follower's belief. The ruling is that it is permissible with conditions. The proof for Qunut in Fajr being abrogated is the hadith of the Prophet's Qunut for a month then leaving it.

Key Terms
قانت (qānit): The one who recites the Qunut supplication, specifically the imam in Witr.
المؤتم (al-mu'tamm): The person praying behind an imam (muqtadi).
القنوت (al-qunūt): A special supplication recited in the Witr prayer after rising from bowing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Qunut is for Witr; following a Shafi'i imam in Witr is permissible if no Hanafi invalidation occurs.

The Hierarchy of Emphasized Sunnah Prayers
Listing the relative strength of Sunnah prayers and the prescribed number of rak'ahs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of emphasized Sunnah prayers according to the Hanafi school and identify the number of rak'ahs for each, including the intra-madhhab difference regarding Jumu'ah.

Core Idea
The two rak'ahs before Fajr are the strongest Sunnah, then Maghrib, then after Zuhr, then after Isha, then before Zuhr.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the relative strength of the Sunnah prayers that the Prophet consistently performed. It provides both the hierarchy and the specific numbers of rak'ahs, with a basis in the hadith of twelve rak'ahs. There is also an important variation within the Hanafi school regarding the number of rak'ahs after Jumu'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing Sunnah prayers before or after the five daily fard prayers. The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized) – the Prophet consistently performed them and encouraged them.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith: 'Whoever perseveres upon twelve rak'ahs in a day and night, Allah will build for him a house in Paradise.' This is the basis for the emphasized Sunnah prayers.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but the Hanafi school still holds it as evidence for sunnah mu'akkadah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'thabara' (perseveres) indicates continuity and emphasis, and the reward of a house in Paradise shows the high merit. This establishes the sunnah mu'akkadah status.
The text does not specify the twelve, but standard Hanafi interpretation includes 2 before Fajr, 4 before Zuhr, 2 after Zuhr, 2 after Maghrib, 2 after Isha.
4
Note the ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school
Regarding the Sunnah after Jumu'ah, Abu Yusuf holds that it is six rak'ahs, while the majority (including Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that it is four.
Abu Yusuf's proof is a narration of the Prophet praying six; the majority's proof is the explicit hadith: 'Whoever prays after Jumu'ah, let him pray four.'
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderant view)
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is four rak'ahs after Jumu'ah, as it is the 'zahir al-riwayah' based on the explicit command. This is also in accordance with the practice of the majority of Companions.
The text states: 'وجه ظاهر الرواية قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: من كان مصلياً بعد الجمعة فليصل أربع ركعات'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسنة قبل الفجر إلخ: ذكر الحلواني أن أقوى السنن ركعتا الفجر ثم سنة المغرب؛ فإنه عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يدعهما في سفر ولا حضر، ثم التي بعد الظهر، فإنها متفق عليها والتي قبلها مختلف فيها، وقيل: هي للفصل بين الأذان والإقامة، ثم التي بعد العشاء، ثم التي قبل الظهر. ونقل في البحر تصحيح أن التي قبل الظهر أكد بعد ركعتي الفجر معللاً بأنه ورد فيها وعيد، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام من ترك الأربع قبل الظهر لم تنله شفاعتي، وقيل: الأربع قبل الظهر والركعتان بعده وبعد المغرب والعشاء كلها سواء، وسنة الفجر أقوى لأنه يكفر جاحد سنيتها. وبعد الظهر والمغرب والعشاء ركعتان، وقبل الظهر والجمعة وبعدها أربع، أي فرضه أي بعد فرضه أي قبل فرضه وندب الأربع قبل العصر والعشاء وبعده، والستة بعد المغرب، استحب رکعتان: هكذا، خبر ست رکعات أي قبلها أربع ركعات المبتدأ، وقدم سنة الفجر؛ لأنها أقوى السنن، ثم سنة المغرب، ثم التي بعد الظهر، ثم التي بعد العشاء، ثم التي قبل الظهر. وقبل الظهر إلخ الأصل في السنن المؤكدة في الصلوات الخمس قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "من ثابر – أي دام - على اثني عشر ركعة في يوم وليلة بنى الله له بيتاً في الجنة"، وفسر على نحو ما ذكر في المتن غير قوله: "بعد الجمعة"، فإنه مذهب عبد الله بن مسعود، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه يصلي بعد الجمعة ستاً؛ لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام صلى ووجه ظاهر الرواية قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "من كان مصلياً بعد الجمعة فليصل أربع ركعات".
And the Sunnah before Fajr etc.: Al-Halwani mentioned that the strongest of the Sunnahs are the two rak'ahs of Fajr, then the Sunnah of Maghrib, because he (peace be upon him) did not abandon them in travel or residence, then those after Zuhr, for they are agreed upon, and those before Zuhr are differed upon. It is said they are for the separation between the adhan and iqamah. Then those after Isha, then those before Zuhr. It is transmitted in al-Bahr that the correction that the four before Zuhr are emphasized after the two of Fajr, reasoned by the warning, which is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever abandons the four before Zuhr will not receive my intercession.' It is also said: the four before Zuhr, the two after it, after Maghrib, and after Isha are all equal. And the Sunnah of Fajr is strongest because disbelief is incurred by the one who denies its Sunnah. And after Zuhr, Maghrib, and Isha are two rak'ahs, and before Zuhr and Jumu'ah and after it are four, meaning after its fard, meaning after its fard, meaning before its fard. And recommended are four before Asr and Isha and after it, and six after Maghrib. It is recommended two rak'ahs: thus, the predicate of six rak'ahs, meaning before it four rak'ahs, the subject. And he presented the Sunnah of Fajr because it is the strongest of Sunnahs, then the Sunnah of Maghrib, then those after Zuhr, then those after Isha, then those before Zuhr. And before Zuhr etc. The basis for the emphasized Sunnahs in the five prayers is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever perseveres – i.e., continues – upon twelve rak'ahs in a day and night, Allah will build for him a house in Paradise.' And it is interpreted in the manner mentioned in the text except his saying 'after Jumu'ah', for that is the madhhab of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. And from Abu Yusuf: he prays after Jumu'ah six, because it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) prayed. The apparent meaning of the narration is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever prays after Jumu'ah, let him pray four rak'ahs.'
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the strongest Sunnah prayers and their specific rak'ahs. It lists the relative strength: the two rak'ahs before Fajr are strongest, then the two after Maghrib, then after Zuhr (agreed upon), then after Isha, then before Zuhr (differed upon). Some say the four before Zuhr are also very strong due to a hadith threat. The matn then states the numbers: after Zuhr, Maghrib, Isha: two rak'ahs each; before Zuhr and Jumu'ah and after Jumu'ah: four rak'ahs each. There is a mention of mandub prayers as well. The basis is the hadith of twelve rak'ahs (two before Fajr, two after Zuhr, two before Zuhr? Actually the standard list: 2 before Fajr, 4 before Zuhr, 2 after Zuhr, 2 after Maghrib, 2 after Isha = 12). The text notes that Abu Yusuf holds six after Jumu'ah, but the relied upon is four.

Key Terms
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah mu'akkadah): An emphasized Sunnah that the Prophet consistently performed and encouraged. Omission is not sinful but loses great reward.
ركعتا الفجر (rak'ata al-fajr): The two rak'ahs of Sunnah before the Fajr fard prayer. Considered the strongest Sunnah prayer.
أربع قبل الظهر (arba' qabl al-zuhr): The four rak'ahs of Sunnah before the Zuhr fard prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The two rak'ahs before Maghrib
B) The two rak'ahs before Fajr
C) The four rak'ahs before Zuhr
D) The two rak'ahs after Isha

Key Takeaway
The two rak'ahs before Fajr are the strongest emphasized Sunnah, followed by Maghrib, after Zuhr, after Isha, and before Zuhr (four rak'ahs), with the number after Jumu'ah being four rak'ahs in the relied-upon view.

Ruling on Congregational Nafl and Inter-Sunnah-Fard Speech
Ruling on congregational nafl and inter-sunnah-fard speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on congregational nafl prayers and explain the effect of speaking between sunnah and fard on the validity and reward of the sunnah prayer.

Core Idea
Congregational nafl is disliked except Tarawih; speaking between sunnah and fard reduces reward but does not invalidate.

The Hanafi school holds that voluntary (nafl) prayers performed in congregation are generally disliked, with a clear exception for Tarawih. Additionally, the period between the sunnah and fard prayers has specific rules: reciting litanies is permissible, but engaging in conversation reduces the reward of the sunnah, though it remains valid. This lesson unpacks these rulings and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act under discussion is performing nafl (voluntary) prayer in congregation. The ruling is that it is disliked (makruh).
The exception is Tarawih, which is explicitly allowed.
2
Note the additional disliked congregation
The passage also mentions Salat al-Raga'ib on the first Friday of Rajab as another example of a disliked congregational nafl.
This is to highlight that any novel congregational nafl is discouraged.
3
Understand the rules for the interval between sunnah and fard
Reciting litanies (awrad) between the sunnah and fard is permissible. Speaking during this interval has consequences.
The passage gives two opinions: the majority view is that reward is reduced; a minority view says the sunnah is invalidated.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon view)
The text presents the reducing-reward view as the apparent position. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The phrase 'لا يسقطها ولكن ينقص ثوابها' indicates the mufta bihi.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab differences
The passage implicitly contrasts with other schools that may permit or recommend certain congregational nafl prayers, such as Salat al-Raga'ib.
Understanding these differences helps in comparative fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة يكره النفل بجماعة إلا التراويح، وكذا الجماعة في أول جمعة من رجب وهي المسماة بصلاة الرغائب، ولا بأس بأن يقرأ بين الفريضة والسنة الأوراد والتكلم بين السنة والفرض لا يسقطها ولكن ينقص ثوابها، وقيل: يسقط.
A supplement: Nafl prayer in congregation is disliked except Tarawih. And likewise congregation on the first Friday of Rajab, which is called Salat al-Raga'ib. There is no harm in reciting litanies between the fard and sunnah prayers. Speaking between the sunnah and fard does not invalidate them but reduces their reward. It is also said it invalidates.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a supplementary ruling (تتمة). It states that performing voluntary (nafl) prayers in congregation is generally disliked (makruh) in the Hanafi school, with the exception of Tarawih which is sunnah. Also, the so-called 'Salat al-Raga'ib' on the first Friday of Rajab is also disliked. Regarding the interval between the sunnah and fard prayers: it is permissible to recite litanies (aurad) between them. If one speaks between the sunnah and fard prayers, the prayer is still valid but the reward is decreased; some scholars say it invalidates the sunnah prayer. The relied upon view is that it does not invalidate but reduces reward.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): Voluntary, supererogatory prayers beyond the five obligatory ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Tarawih
C) Tahajjud
D) Duha

Key Takeaway
Congregational nafl is disliked except Tarawih; speaking between sunnah and fard reduces reward but does not invalidate.
